TCEHY (Tencent Holdings) — Feynman Explanation

Problem Type: Regime/Political

1. What This Company Actually Does

Imagine if WhatsApp, Apple Pay, the App Store, Shopify, and your local government's citizen portal were all fused into a single app that 1.4 billion people used every single day — and that app was called WeChat. That's Tencent's foundation. On top of that, Tencent built the world's largest gaming empire: they made Honor of Kings (the most-played mobile game on earth), they own League of Legends, and they publish PUBG Mobile. If you've ever tapped a phone to pay for street food in Shanghai, clicked into a mini-app to book a doctor's appointment, or watched a livestream on a Chinese platform — Tencent's infrastructure was almost certainly underneath it.

2. What Kind of Problem This Is

This is a Regime/Political problem. Full stop.

Here's why that label matters so much: everything you're about to read about Tencent's quality is true. The business is genuinely excellent. The numbers are not cooked. The moat is real. But none of that is the crux of the investment decision — and if you treat it as a clean analytical problem, you will be reasoning correctly about the wrong question.

In 2021, Tencent was thriving. Gaming revenues were growing, WeChat was indispensable, and the stock was near all-time highs. Then the Chinese Communist Party decided that children were spending too much time gaming. In a matter of weeks, regulators froze new game approvals for months, banned minors from gaming on weekdays entirely, and capped weekend play at three hours. Tencent's stock fell roughly 50% from peak to trough — not because the business deteriorated, but because the state restructured it overnight.

That is what "regime risk" means. It is not the ordinary risk of a bad product cycle or a new competitor. It is the risk that the rules of ownership themselves are subject to change by a sovereign actor who has demonstrated both the will and the legal authority to act.

This is the defining feature of investing in a Chinese technology platform in 2024: you are not simply buying a great business. You are buying a great business that operates under a lease arrangement with the Chinese state. The state has not cancelled the lease. It may never cancel it. But the terms are not yours to negotiate, and the landlord has shown it will rewrite them when it judges this necessary. The 36% discount to global peers — 18.3x P/E versus 28.9x — is the market's honest pricing of that lease arrangement. It is not an error. It is a risk premium.

Understanding this does not mean you avoid the investment. It means you size it for the jurisdiction.

3. How to Think About This Kind of Problem

When the dominant risk is political rather than competitive, you need a different mental toolkit. Here is a reusable framework in four steps:

Step 1: Separate business quality from political safety. These are two independent variables. A business can be world-class on quality and still be uninvestable — or investable at the right price. Tencent scores extremely high on quality. Political safety is a separate score, and it is lower. Never conflate them. Investors who bought Chinese internet stocks in 2020 often conflated them, which is why the 2021 crackdown was so painful.

Step 2: Ask "what has the state already done?" The track record tells you more than the rhetoric. China's government cracked down on gaming for minors, halted Ant Group's IPO, restructured Didi immediately after its US listing, and fined Alibaba $2.8 billion. That is a revealed preference. The question is not "could they act?" — they already did. The question is "how far did they go, and did the business survive?" In Tencent's case, the business survived, adapted, and resumed growth. That matters.

Step 3: Ask "is the state aligned or misaligned with this business continuing?" WeChat is now critical infrastructure for Chinese society — government services run through it, digital payments flow through it, and 1.4 billion people organize their daily lives on it. That creates a form of structural protection: the state cannot destroy WeChat without destroying enormous economic and social utility it depends on. This is a different position than Didi (a taxi app that competed with state interests) or Ant Group (a private credit platform that threatened state banking). Tencent is more embedded, more useful to the system. That does not make it safe — it makes it less unsafe.

Step 4: Size for the scenario where you are wrong about the regime. Position sizing is the mechanism that resolves this dilemma. If the regime stays stable, a 4-5% position at a 36% discount to peers generates excellent returns. If the regime turns adversarial — forced restructuring, VIE structure challenge, delisting — a 4-5% position is a recoverable loss. You are not betting the portfolio on the regime's goodwill. You are taking a measured, asymmetric bet where the upside justifies the downside at this size. The conviction is moderate, not high. That is honest. Size accordingly.

4. How They Make Money

Tencent has four engines, and they are all running:

  • Gaming ($109B revenue base, dominant contributor): Honor of Kings and PUBG Mobile generate billions through in-app purchases. International gaming grew 43% last year. This is a hits-driven business with global reach.
  • Advertising ($21% growth): WeChat's 1.4 billion users see AI-targeted ads in their feeds and on mini-programs. As AI targeting improves, ad revenue per user rises — and Tencent is just beginning to monetize this asset.
  • FinTech & Business Services (10% growth): WeChat Pay processes hundreds of millions of daily transactions. Tencent takes a small fee on each one. This is a toll road on the Chinese digital economy.
  • Cloud (22% growth, now profitable): Late to cloud relative to Alibaba, but growing fast and finally generating real margins.

Net margin of 29.4% means nearly 30 cents of every dollar in revenue becomes profit. Free cash flow is $23.8 billion — more than most US tech companies that trade at much higher multiples. Stock-based compensation is 3.1% of revenue versus a peer average of 13.7%, meaning the earnings are real and shareholders are not being diluted to subsidize growth.

5. Why They're Hard to Kill

Think about what it would take to displace WeChat in China. You would need to convince 1.4 billion people to simultaneously abandon their chat history, their payment wallets, their mini-app subscriptions, their government service logins, and their social networks — and move to something new. That is not a product problem that a competitor solves. That is a coordination problem that is essentially impossible.

This is what economists call a network effect with switching costs layered on top. Each additional user makes WeChat more valuable to every existing user (network effect). And each additional service you run through WeChat — your utility bills, your doctor, your bank — makes it more painful to leave (switching cost). Tencent has woven itself into the daily infrastructure of Chinese life so thoroughly that "kill Tencent" and "disrupt Chinese society" have become nearly synonymous. That is a moat.

ROIC of 16.7% confirms the moat is real, not just a story. A company with genuine competitive advantages earns returns on capital well above its cost of capital, year after year. Tencent does.

6. The One Thing That Could Go Wrong

The single biggest risk is not a competitor, not a product failure, not an economic recession: it is a direct challenge to the VIE structure — the legal arrangement that allows foreign investors to hold economic interests in Chinese tech companies. Chinese law prohibits direct foreign ownership of internet businesses, so Tencent and its peers use Variable Interest Entities (VIEs), a contractual workaround that has never been fully tested in Chinese courts. If Beijing decided tomorrow that VIE contracts are unenforceable, or if a US-China political rupture triggered forced delisting of Chinese ADRs from US exchanges, the value of TCEHY shares held by foreign investors could be impaired in ways that have nothing to do with how well the business performs. The US DoD military designation is an early signal of this geopolitical friction. This is low-probability but high-severity — exactly the risk that justifies moderate rather than high conviction.

7. The Price Tag Problem

Imagine two houses on the same street. The first house — call it the American Tech house — is beautifully maintained, priced at $1.45 million, and has a clear title with every legal protection you'd expect. The second house — the Tencent house — is larger, generates more rental income, has a stronger foundation, and is priced at $920,000. Why the discount?

Because the Tencent house sits in a jurisdiction where the local authority can, under certain circumstances, change the zoning laws overnight, limit how many tenants you can have, or reclassify part of the property for public use. It has happened before — not to your specific house, but to a neighbor's. The authority did not demolish it. They just reshaped it. The house survived and kept generating income. But the title is not as clean as the American house.

At $64.33, TCEHY is priced at 18.3x earnings — 36% cheaper than global tech peers at 28.9x. That discount is not the market being foolish. It is the market correctly pricing the jurisdictional lease risk. The investment case is not "the market is wrong about the discount." The investment case is "the discount is large enough to compensate for the risk, and the business quality is high enough that you are being paid well to accept it."

The buy zone is $54–$68. The current price is inside it. If you can look at this clearly — a premium business, a real discount, a manageable but non-trivial political risk, a position size that lets you sleep — then the math works. If you cannot accept the jurisdiction, no price is right. That is not a character flaw. It is just an honest assessment of your own risk tolerance, which is the beginning of all good investing.

Reality Check

A red team critique from the historical personas of Munger and Buffett.

THE RED TEAM AUDIT: TENCENT (TCEHY) at $64.33

PART ONE: THE GHOST OF CHARLIE MUNGER

[Adjusts glasses, scowls at the report]

On Stock-Based Compensation — "Rat Poison Squared"

You know, I've spent decades watching corporate managements hand themselves the shareholders' money and then tell everyone to ignore it because it's "non-cash." It's the most disgusting sleight of hand in modern finance.

The Council says SBC is 3.1% of revenue. Sounds modest, doesn't it? Like a small tip at a restaurant. But Burry — and I don't agree with that man on everything, but he's not stupid — points out it's 87% of free cash flow. That means for every dollar of cash Tencent generates, eighty-seven cents gets shoveled back to insiders in the form of dilutive stock grants. The buybacks aren't returning capital to shareholders — they're running in place to offset dilution. That's not a capital return program, that's a treadmill.

Now, is 87% of FCF the right framing? Let me check. FCF is $23.8B, SBC is $20.7B. That ratio is $20.7/$23.8 = 87%. The math checks out. The Council's response? They point to "Owner Earnings" of $29.6B. That's a Buffett concept they're misapplying. Owner Earnings ADD BACK SBC? Then you're double-counting. You can't give management stock, buy it back, and pretend neither transaction happened. Pick one: either SBC is a real cost, or buybacks are a real return. You don't get to ignore both.

The 4.8% share decline is real, I'll grant that. Shares are actually shrinking. But at what cost? They're spending billions buying back shares that they're simultaneously issuing. It's like bailing water out of a boat while your partner drills holes in the hull.

Munger Verdict on SBC: The Council underweights this. It's not disqualifying — plenty of great businesses have SBC — but pretending it's 3.1% when it eats 87% of FCF is intellectual dishonesty. Grade: D on this metric.

On EBITDA and Non-IFRS Adjustments

Every time I hear "adjusted EBITDA" I reach for my revolver. EBITDA was invented by a generation of bankers who wanted to pretend that depreciation, amortization, interest, and taxes don't exist. As I've said: "I think that every time you see the word EBITDA, you should substitute the words 'bullshit earnings.'"

Tencent reports under IFRS and applies "non-IFRS adjustments." These are standard for Chinese tech — stripping out SBC, amortization of intangibles from acquisitions, and investment fair value changes. The question is whether the underlying IFRS numbers support the thesis. Net margin at 29.4% on IFRS is legitimately strong. The P/E of 18.3x — if that's on IFRS earnings — is defensible. But if it's on non-IFRS earnings, you're paying for a fiction.

Munger Verdict: Demand IFRS P/E. If 18.3x is IFRS, acceptable. If non-IFRS, add 2-4 turns mentally.

On "Too Hard"

This is the most important question the Council didn't adequately address.

I spent my career with a "Too Hard" pile, and I put most things in it. China tech should make any honest analyst pause. Here's why:

  1. VIE Structure: You don't own Tencent. You own a Cayman Islands shell company with a contractual claim on profits. This is a legal fiction that the CCP tolerates. The word "grandfathered" gives me no comfort — grandfather clauses exist until the grandfather decides they don't. Xi Jinping is not your grandfather.

  2. CCP Regulatory Risk: In 2021, the CCP wiped $1 trillion off Chinese tech in a matter of months. They froze game approvals for Tencent. They limited children's gaming hours. They investigated Ant Financial into oblivion. The Council notes "Pony Ma met Xi early 2025" as if this is bullish. You know what I call a business whose survival depends on meetings with an authoritarian leader? A concession, not a company.

  3. US DoD List: Tencent is on the U.S. Department of Defense list of Chinese military companies. This isn't a trivia fact — it's a potential trigger for forced divestiture by U.S. funds, sanctions escalation, or CFIUS blocking future deals. The Council mentions this like a footnote. It should be in bold, underlined, and circled in red.

  4. Knowability: Can I model the probability of a CCP crackdown? No. Can I model Xi Jinping's political calculus? No. Can I price VIE risk? No. When you can't model the risk, you can't price the asset. When you can't price the asset, it goes in the Too Hard pile. Period.

I once said: "It's not supposed to be easy. Anyone who finds it easy is stupid." China tech isn't just hard — it's unknowable in the ways that matter most.

Munger Verdict on Too Hard: This belongs in the Too Hard pile for most investors. The regulatory risk is not a tail risk — it's an existential variable that cannot be modeled. Grade: F on knowability.

On Pricing Power

Can Tencent raise prices? Let's decompose:

  • Gaming: Moderate. They have Honour of Kings and other dominant titles, but games are hit-driven and youth regulations cap monetization. You can't raise prices when the government is watching how much money kids spend.
  • WeChat: Strong on the ad side — 21% ad growth suggests pricing power. But WeChat doesn't charge users. The pricing power is indirect, through merchants and advertisers. That's real but one step removed.
  • Cloud: Weak. Cloud is commoditized, they just turned profitable for the first time, and they're competing with Alibaba and Huawei. You don't have pricing power when you're the #3 player celebrating your first quarter of profitability.
  • Fintech: Regulated. WeChat Pay pricing is increasingly subject to government guidance.

Munger Verdict on Pricing Power: Moderate. WeChat's ad pricing power is real. Gaming is constrained. Cloud and fintech are weak. This isn't See's Candies. Grade: C+.

Overall Munger Assessment

The business is genuinely good. WeChat is extraordinary. The diversification is real. 29.4% net margins and 16.7% ROIC are world-class. But the SBC is being minimized, the VIE risk is being hand-waved, and the CCP overhang makes this a coin flip between "cheap high-quality business" and "your equity goes to zero because a man in Beijing had a bad Tuesday."

"Invert, always invert." What kills this investment? The CCP. Can I control or predict that? No. Therefore I cannot invest.

If I were still alive, I'd say: "The business is wonderful. The stock is cheap. And I wouldn't touch it with a ten-foot pole."

PART TWO: WARREN BUFFETT'S AUDIT

[Leans back, sips Cherry Coke, smiles warmly]

Circle of Competence

Well, let me start by saying something that won't surprise anyone who knows me: I sold our Tencent stake. BYD was different — that was Charlie's idea, and we understood batteries and cars. But Tencent? Let me be honest.

Do I understand what WeChat does? Broadly, yes. It's a toll bridge. 1.4 billion people use it to message, pay, shop, and live their digital lives. That's extraordinary. I understand toll bridges — we owned BNSF railroad, we own Geico, we understand businesses where the customer has no practical alternative.

But here's where my circle of competence ends: I don't understand the Chinese regulatory environment well enough to sleep at night. I invested in PetroChina years ago — bought it cheap, sold it for a nice profit. But PetroChina was simple: oil in the ground, priced in dollars. Tencent is a technology-regulation-politics triple helix, and I'd be lying if I said I could predict how those three strands interact.

The VIE structure troubles me particularly. We own stocks because we own a piece of the business. With a VIE, you own a contract. A contract governed by Chinese law. Enforced by Chinese courts. In a country where the rule of law is... well, let's say it's different from Omaha.

Circle of Competence Verdict: Out. Not because the business is unknowable — it's because the political risk is unknowable, and that's the variable that determines whether your equity is worth $64 or $0.

The Toll Bridge Question

Now, let me give Tencent credit where it's due, because I believe in being fair.

WeChat is one of the finest toll bridges I've ever seen. 1.4 billion monthly active users. 949 million using Mini Programs. If you want to do business in China, you go through WeChat. That's a toll bridge.

But I'd make three observations:

First, the tollkeeper doesn't own the bridge. The CCP owns the bridge. Tencent operates it at the pleasure of the government. In 2021, the government decided the toll was too high and knocked it down. They could do it again tomorrow. My toll bridges — the ones I like to own — they come with durable franchises in jurisdictions where property rights are respected. WeChat's franchise depends on one phone call from Zhongnanhai.

Second, ByteDance and Douyin are the first credible threat to WeChat's attention monopoly in a decade. 33.5% of users under 24 is a good sign for WeChat, but I'd want to see where those under-24s are spending their incremental time. If the answer is Douyin, then WeChat's toll bridge has a competing highway being built next to it.

Third, the international gaming business (+43%) is genuinely impressive and somewhat insulated from CCP risk. That's the piece I find most attractive — but it's not yet large enough to justify the whole valuation.

Toll Bridge Verdict: Real but landlord risk. WeChat is a magnificent bridge operated by a tenant who can be evicted.

Capital Preservation — Rule Number One

"Rule #1: Never lose money. Rule #2: Never forget Rule #1."

Let me walk through the downside scenario:

  • CCP crackdown 2.0: Gaming restrictions tighten, fintech regulated into a utility, antitrust fines. Precedent: 2021. Impact: 30-50% drawdown overnight.
  • US-China decoupling: DoD list escalation, forced divestiture by US funds, ADR delisting risk. Impact: Liquidity crisis, 20-40% drawdown.
  • VIE nullification: Low probability but catastrophic. Impact: Equity goes to zero.
  • Prosus selling: From 29% to 23% and likely continuing. That's persistent supply overhang.
  • DPO at 139 days: Up from 107. That means Tencent is squeezing its suppliers harder to inflate cash flow. When DPO normalizes, Burry's right — that's a $13 billion headwind to FCF. Real FCF yield drops from 5.1% to something much less appetizing.

At $64.33, you're getting a 5.1% owner yield assuming everything goes right. If DPO normalizes and SBC is treated as real, your yield is closer to 1-2%. For that kind of return, I can buy Treasury bills and sleep like a baby.

Capital Preservation Verdict: Unacceptable risk-reward. The downside scenarios are severe and unhedgeable. Even at 18.3x earnings, you're not being adequately compensated for the political risk.

Buffett's Final Word

You know, people always ask me what I think about China. Here's what I'll say: China has wonderful businesses and brilliant entrepreneurs. Tencent is, by any operating measure, a world-class company. If this business were headquartered in Delaware and governed by U.S. law, I'd be very interested at this price.

But it's not in Delaware. It's in Shenzhen, and the real controlling shareholder isn't in the boardroom — he's in Beijing. When the most important variable in your investment thesis is the mood of an authoritarian government, you're not investing. You're speculating. And at Berkshire, we don't speculate.

I'd rather miss a double than risk a zero.

PART THREE: THE "OLD SCHOOL" VERDICT

Would Berkshire Actually Buy This?

No. And we didn't — we invested through intermediaries (BYD via Charlie's conviction, PetroChina as a commodity play) and avoided direct Chinese tech equity. The VIE structure alone disqualifies it under Berkshire's ownership principles. We want to own businesses, not contracts.

Graham-Dodd Analysis

Let me apply Benjamin Graham's framework honestly:

Criterion Assessment Pass/Fail
Adequate size $580B market cap. Yes. PASS
Strong financial condition Debt 358B CNY, but cash-generative. Acceptable. PASS
Earnings stability 10+ years of profits, diversified. PASS
Dividend record Pays dividends, growing. PASS
Earnings growth 15% revenue growth, strong. PASS
Moderate P/E 18.3x vs 28.9x peers. PASS
Moderate P/B Reasonable for tech. PASS
Margin of safety At $64 vs $54-68 buy zone? Thin. MARGINAL
Quality of earnings SBC at 87% of FCF. DPO inflation. FAIL
Governance & ownership rights VIE. CCP. No real ownership. FAIL

Graham would note that the quantitative metrics pass, but the qualitative factors — governance, ownership rights, regulatory predictability — fail catastrophically. Graham said: "The margin of safety is always dependent on the price paid." But no margin of safety is wide enough when you can't trust the property rights.

Letter Grade: C+

The operating business merits a B+ to A-. The financial metrics are strong. But Graham-Dodd investing isn't just about numbers — it's about safety of principal, and VIE + CCP risk makes safety of principal unknowable.

FINAL SCORECARD

Dimension Rating
Circle of Competence OUT — Political risk unmodellable
Moat Integrity Wide but Landlord-Dependent — WeChat is a real moat; the CCP is the landlord
Management Character Owners — Pony Ma is aligned, buybacks are real (net of SBC, debatable)
Berkshire Buy? PASS — Not No, but Pass. Too Hard pile.
Graham-Dodd Grade C+ — Quantitative pass, qualitative fail

DISSENT FROM THE COUNCIL

The Silicon Council voted 11-1 BUY. We, the Red Team, dissent. Our dissent rests on three pillars:

  1. SBC is understated. The Council presents 3.1% of revenue. Burry presents 87% of FCF. The truth matters, and the Council chose the flattering number.

  2. CCP risk is unpriced. No discount rate adequately compensates for the risk that an authoritarian government can restructure your equity to zero. The Council treats this as a "risk factor." We treat it as the central question.

  3. DPO manipulation inflates FCF. Moving from 107 to 139 days payable generates one-time cash flow that the Council is capitalizing as a perpetuity. When this normalizes, $13B evaporates from FCF, and Burry's 0.5% real yield becomes the operative number.

Our recommendation: PASS. Not because the business is bad — it's genuinely excellent. But because the price does not compensate for the unhedgeable, unmodellable, existential political risk. If forced to put a price on it, we'd want a 40% discount to the Council's fair value — entry below $40 — to create a margin of safety wide enough to absorb VIE risk.

TENCENT (TCEHY) — Dinner Table Edition

Silicon Council Dispatch | April 2026

1. The "Napkin" Pitch

Imagine if Mark Zuckerberg owned WhatsApp, Apple Pay, the App Store, Fortnite, and your local government's website — and 1.4 billion people used all of them daily through a single app called WeChat. That's Tencent. They make money every time someone chats, pays a bill, buys a game skin, or hails a cab in China.

2. The Good News

The Moat (Protection)

WeChat isn't just an app — it's infrastructure. In China, you use WeChat to pay your rent, renew your driver's license, order lunch, and chat with your mom. Switching away from it isn't like deleting Instagram. It's like trying to opt out of having a phone number. That's what investors call a moat — a feature so deeply dug into daily life that competitors can't get across it.

The Secret Sauce

Three growth engines firing at once: - Gaming: Honor of Kings, League of Legends, PUBG Mobile — and international titles growing +43%. (Yes, your teenager is funding this.) - AI & Cloud: AI spending doubled; their cloud division just turned profitable. Late to the party, but a very well-funded guest. - Financial discipline: Stock-based compensation (the practice of paying employees with shares, which dilutes your ownership) is 3.1% of revenue. The typical tech company does 13.7%. Translation: management isn't giving the house away.

Profit margins sit at 29.4% — meaning for every dollar that comes in, nearly 30 cents stays as profit. Most retailers dream of 5%.

3. The Bad News

The "Banana Peel" Risks

  • The CCP Factor: The Chinese government has previously fined Tencent billions and frozen new game approvals. They can do it again, whenever they want, for whatever reason they want. This is not hypothetical — it already happened. You are investing in a company that operates at the pleasure of a government that does not answer to shareholders.

  • The Overhang Seller: Prosus (a Dutch holding company) owns a massive stake and has been steadily selling shares to fund its own buybacks. Think of it as a large neighbor who keeps listing yard sale items — puts a lid on how fast the price can rise.

  • The US DoD List: Tencent is on the U.S. Department of Defense's list of "Chinese Military Companies." This doesn't ban Americans from owning it, but it scares institutional money away and could escalate.

  • ByteDance (TikTok's parent): The one competitor that actually worries Tencent. Short-form video is eating time that used to belong to WeChat Mini Programs and games.

4. The "Price Tag" Logic

Think of buying stock like buying a house.

Label Price What it means
The Steal $54 The house is basically on fire — but the bones are great. If you can stomach the smoke, this is the deal of the decade.
Fair Value $68 Priced like a normal house in a normal neighborhood. You're not robbing anyone; no one's robbing you.
Current Price $64.33 You're buying a well-maintained house at a modest discount — because the neighbors (investors) are nervous about the zoning laws (CCP).

The math in plain English: Tencent's peers trade at 28.9x earnings (what investors pay per dollar of profit). Tencent trades at 18.3x. That gap — call it the "China Discount" — is either a genuine danger premium or a screaming opportunity, depending on how you weigh political risk.

The Council says: at $64.33, you're paying less than fair value for a business that is not, by most measures, broken.

5. The Final Verdict

BUY — with eyes wide open.

The Silicon Council voted 11–1 in favor. (One expert remains unconvinced — probably the one who reads geopolitical risk reports for fun.)

Buy Zone: $54–$68. At the current price of $64.33, you're inside the zone — not at the screaming-deal end, but not overpaying either.

Suggested position size: 4–5% of portfolio. This is not a "bet the farm" situation. It's a "plant one row and watch it closely" situation.

Bull case: 48–63% upside over three years if the political clouds don't darken further.

The one-liner: WeChat has 1.4 billion daily users, six profitable businesses, and trades cheap because people are scared of Beijing. If you believe the fear is overdone — and 11 of our 12 experts do — this is the kind of discount that shows up once in a cycle.

As Munger would say: "All I want to know is where I'm going to die, so I'll never go there." For Tencent, the death scenario is CCP confiscation. Bet on whether that probability is priced at 5% or 50%.
